The provinces of <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a> maintain their own provincial police forces, the <a href="/wiki/Ontario_Provincial_Police" title="Ontario Provincial Police">Ontario Provincial Police</a> (OPP), and the <a href="/wiki/S%C3%BBret%C3%A9_du_Qu%C3%A9bec" title="Sûreté du Québec">Sûreté du Québec</a> (SQ). Policing in <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Newfoundland and Labrador">Newfoundland and Labrador</a> is provided by the RCMP, and the RNC. The aforementioned services also provides municipal policing, although larger Canadian municipalities may establish their own police service.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lebanon">Lebanon</span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>, the current police force were established in 1861, with creation of the <a href="/wiki/Gendarmerie" title="Gendarmerie">Gendarmerie</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="India_2">India</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gcp_patrol_car.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Gcp_patrol_car.jpg/220px-Gcp_patrol_car.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Gcp_patrol_car.jpg/330px-Gcp_patrol_car.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Gcp_patrol_car.jpg/440px-Gcp_patrol_car.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gcp_patrol_car.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Policemen on patrol in <i>Khaki</i> uniform in the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Chennai_Police" title="Greater Chennai Police">Greater Chennai Police</a> patrol car in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, the police are under the control of respective <a href="/wiki/States_and_union_territories_of_India" title="States and union territories of India">States and union territories</a> and is known to be under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_Police_Services_(India)" title="State Police Services (India)">State Police Services</a> (SPS). The candidates selected for the SPS are usually posted as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deputy_Superintendent_of_Police" title="Deputy Superintendent of Police">Deputy Superintendent of Police</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assistant_Commissioner_of_Police" title="Assistant Commissioner of Police">Assistant Commissioner of Police</a> once their probationary period ends. On prescribed satisfactory service in the SPS, the officers are nominated to the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Police_Service" title="Indian Police Service">Indian Police Service</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The service color is usually dark blue and red, while the uniform color is <i>Khaki</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Law_enforcement_in_the_United_States" title="Law enforcement in the United States">Law enforcement in the United States</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Colonial_history_of_the_United_States" title="Colonial history of the United States">Colonial America</a>, the county sheriff was the most important law enforcement official. For instance, the New York Sheriff's Office was founded in 1626, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albany_County_Sheriff%27s_Department_(New_York)" title="Albany County Sheriff's Department (New York)">Albany County Sheriff's Department</a> in the 1660s. The county sheriff, who was an elected official, was responsible for enforcing laws, collecting taxes, supervising elections, and handling the legal business of the county government. Sheriffs would investigate crimes and make arrests after citizens filed complaints or provided information about a crime, but did not carry out patrols or otherwise take preventive action. Villages and cities typically hired constables and marshals, who were empowered to make arrests and serve warrants. Many municipalities also formed a night watch, or group of citizen volunteers who would patrol the streets at night looking for crime or fires. Typically, constables and marshals were the main law enforcement officials available during the day while the night watch would serve during the night. Eventually, municipalities formed day watch groups. Rioting was handled by local militias.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1700s, the <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Carolina" title="Province of Carolina">Province of Carolina</a> (later <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North</a>- and <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a>) established <a href="/wiki/Slave_patrol" title="Slave patrol">slave patrols</a> in order to prevent slave rebellions and enslaved people from escaping.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>  By 1785 the <a href="/wiki/Charleston,_South_Carolina" title="Charleston, South Carolina">Charleston</a> Guard and Watch had "a distinct <a href="/wiki/Command_hierarchy" title="Command hierarchy">chain of command</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uniform" title="Uniform">uniforms</a>, sole responsibility for policing, <a href="/wiki/Salary" title="Salary">salary</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Authorized_use_of_force" title="Authorized use of force">authorized use of force</a>, and a focus on <a href="/wiki/Crime_prevention" title="Crime prevention">preventing crime</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1789 the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marshals_Service" title="United States Marshals Service">United States Marshals Service</a> was established, followed by other federal services such as the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Park_Police" title="United States Park Police">U.S. Parks Police</a> (1791)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Mint_Police" title="United States Mint Police">U.S. Mint Police</a> (1792).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> The first city police services were established in <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Police_Department" title="Philadelphia Police Department">Philadelphia</a> in 1751,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Richmond,_Virginia" title="Richmond, Virginia">Richmond, Virginia</a> in 1807,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a> in 1838,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York</a> in 1845.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Secret_Service" title="U.S. Secret Service">U.S. Secret Service</a> was founded in 1865 and was for some time the main investigative body for the federal government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:U.S._Marshals_knock_and_announce.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="598" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/U.S._Marshals_knock_and_announce.jpg/170px-U.S._Marshals_knock_and_announce.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/U.S._Marshals_knock_and_announce.jpg/255px-U.S._Marshals_knock_and_announce.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/U.S._Marshals_knock_and_announce.jpg/340px-U.S._Marshals_knock_and_announce.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:U.S._Marshals_knock_and_announce.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Deputy <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marshals_Service" title="United States Marshals Service">U.S. Marshal</a> covers his fellow officers with an <a href="/wiki/M4_carbine" title="M4 carbine">M4 carbine</a> during a "<a href="/wiki/Knock-and-announce" title="Knock-and-announce">knock-and-announce</a>" procedure</div></div></div>
<p>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Old_West" title="American Old West">American Old West</a>, law enforcement was carried out by local sheriffs, rangers, constables, and federal marshals. There were also town marshals responsible for serving civil and criminal warrants, maintaining the jails, and carrying out arrests for petty crime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>In recent years, in addition to federal, state, and local forces, some <a href="/wiki/Special_district_(United_States)" title="Special district (United States)">special districts</a> have been formed to provide extra police protection in designated areas. These districts may be known as neighborhood improvement districts, crime prevention districts, or security districts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_of_theory">Development of theory</span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Michel_Foucault" title="Michel Foucault">Michel Foucault</a> wrote that the contemporary concept of police as a paid and funded functionary of the state was developed by German and French legal scholars and practitioners in <a href="/wiki/Public_administration" title="Public administration">public administration</a> and <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">statistics</a> in the 17th and early 18th centuries, most notably with Nicolas Delamare's <i><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20081112154147/http://cujas.synasoft.fr/page.asp?ouvrage=225" rel="nofollow">Traité de la Police</a></i> ("Treatise on the Police"), first published in 1705. The German <i><a href="/wiki/Polizeiwissenschaft" title="Polizeiwissenschaft">Polizeiwissenschaft</a></i> (Science of Police) first theorized by <a href="/wiki/Philipp_von_H%C3%B6rnigk" title="Philipp von Hörnigk">Philipp von Hörnigk</a>, a 17th-century Austrian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_economist" title="Political economist">political economist</a> and civil servant, and much more famously by <a href="/wiki/Johann_Heinrich_Gottlob_Justi" title="Johann Heinrich Gottlob Justi">Johann Heinrich Gottlob Justi</a>, who produced an important theoretical work known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cameral" title="Cameral">Cameral</a> science on the formulation of police.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Foucault cites <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Magdalene_Humpert&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Magdalene Humpert (page does not exist)">Magdalene Humpert</a> author of <i>Bibliographie der Kameralwissenschaften</i> (1937) in which the author makes note of a substantial bibliography was produced of over 4,000 pieces of the practice of <i>Polizeiwissenschaft</i>.  However, this may be a mistranslation of Foucault's own work since the actual source of Magdalene Humpert states over 14,000 items were produced from the 16th century dates ranging from 1520 to 1850.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>As conceptualized by the <i>Polizeiwissenschaft</i>, according to Foucault the police had an administrative, economic and social duty ("procuring abundance"). It was in charge of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demographics" title="Demographics">demographic</a> concerns<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness"><span title=" (February 2021)">vague</span></a></i>]</sup> and needed to be incorporated within the western political philosophy system of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raison_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Raison d'état">raison d'état</a> and therefore giving the superficial appearance of empowering the <a href="/wiki/Population" title="Population">population</a> (and unwittingly supervising the population), which, according to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mercantilist" title="Mercantilist">mercantilist</a> theory, was to be the main strength of the <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">state</a>. Thus, its functions largely overreached simple law enforcement activities and included <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">public health</a> concerns, <a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">urban planning</a> (which was important because of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miasma_theory_of_disease" title="Miasma theory of disease">miasma theory of disease</a>; thus, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cemeteries" title="Cemeteries">cemeteries</a> were moved out of town, etc.), and surveillance of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prices" title="Prices">prices</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jeremy_Bentham_by_Henry_William_Pickersgill_detail.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="728" data-file-width="536" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Jeremy_Bentham_by_Henry_William_Pickersgill_detail.jpg/150px-Jeremy_Bentham_by_Henry_William_Pickersgill_detail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Jeremy_Bentham_by_Henry_William_Pickersgill_detail.jpg/225px-Jeremy_Bentham_by_Henry_William_Pickersgill_detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Jeremy_Bentham_by_Henry_William_Pickersgill_detail.jpg/300px-Jeremy_Bentham_by_Henry_William_Pickersgill_detail.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jeremy_Bentham_by_Henry_William_Pickersgill_detail.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Bentham" title="Jeremy Bentham">Jeremy Bentham</a>, philosopher who advocated for the establishment of preventive police forces and influenced the reforms of Sir <a href="/wiki/Robert_Peel" title="Robert Peel">Robert Peel</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The concept of preventive policing, or policing to deter crime from taking place, gained influence in the late 18th century. Police Magistrate <a href="/wiki/John_Fielding" title="John Fielding">John Fielding</a>, head of the <a href="/wiki/Bow_Street_Runners" title="Bow Street Runners">Bow Street Runners</a>, argued that "...it is much better to prevent even one man from being a rogue than apprehending and bringing forty to justice."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marin_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marin-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Utilitarianism" title="Utilitarianism">Utilitarian</a> philosopher, <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Bentham" title="Jeremy Bentham">Jeremy Bentham</a>, promoted the views of <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cesare,_Marquis_of_Beccaria" title="Cesare, Marquis of Beccaria">Marquis Cesare Beccaria</a>, and disseminated a translated version of "Essay on Crime in Punishment". Bentham espoused the guiding principle of "the greatest good for the greatest number":
</p>
<blockquote><p>It is better to prevent crimes than to punish them. This is the chief aim of every good system of legislation, which is the art of leading men to the greatest possible happiness or to the least possible misery, according to calculation of all the goods and evils of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marin_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marin-94">[94]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Patrick_Colquhoun" title="Patrick Colquhoun">Patrick Colquhoun</a>'s influential work, <i>A Treatise on the Police of the Metropolis</i> (1797) was heavily influenced by Benthamite thought. Colquhoun's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_Police_Force" title="Marine Police Force">Thames River Police</a> was founded on these principles, and in contrast to the <a href="/wiki/Bow_Street_Runners" title="Bow Street Runners">Bow Street Runners</a>, acted as a deterrent by their continual presence on the riverfront, in addition to being able to intervene if they spotted a crime in progress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Edwin_Chadwick" title="Edwin Chadwick">Edwin Chadwick</a>'s 1829 article, "Preventive police" in the <i>London Review</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> argued that prevention ought to be the <i>primary</i> concern of a police body, which was not the case in practice. The reason, argued Chadwick, was that "A preventive police would act more immediately by placing difficulties in obtaining the objects of temptation." In contrast to a deterrent of punishment, a preventive police force would deter criminality by making crime cost-ineffective – "crime doesn't pay". In the second draft of his 1829 Police Act, the "object" of the new Metropolitan Police, was changed by Robert Peel to the "principal object," which was the "prevention of crime."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Later historians would attribute the perception of England's "appearance of orderliness and love of public order" to the preventive principle entrenched in Peel's police system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>Development of modern police forces around the world was contemporary to the formation of the state, later defined by sociologist <a href="/wiki/Max_Weber" title="Max Weber">Max Weber</a> as achieving a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monopoly_on_the_legitimate_use_of_physical_force" title="Monopoly on the legitimate use of physical force">monopoly on the legitimate use of physical force</a>" and which was primarily exercised by the police and the <a href="/wiki/Military" title="Military">military</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marxist" title="Marxist">Marxist</a> theory situates the development of the modern state as part of the rise of capitalism, in which the police are one component of the <a href="/wiki/Bourgeoisie" title="Bourgeoisie">bourgeoisie</a>'s repressive apparatus for subjugating the <a href="/wiki/Working_class" title="Working class">working class</a>. By contrast, the <a href="/wiki/Peelian_principles" title="Peelian principles">Peelian principles</a> argue that "the power of the police...is dependent on public approval of their existence, actions and behavior", a philosophy known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Policing_by_consent" title="Policing by consent">policing by consent</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personnel_and_organization">Personnel and organization</span></h2>
<p>Police forces include both preventive (uniformed) police and <a href="/wiki/Detective" title="Detective">detectives</a>. Terminology varies from country to country. Police functions include protecting life and property, enforcing <a href="/wiki/Criminal_law" title="Criminal law">criminal law</a>, criminal investigations, regulating traffic, crowd control, public safety duties, civil defense, emergency management, searching for missing persons, lost property and other duties concerned with public order. Regardless of size, police forces are generally organized as a <a href="/wiki/Hierarchy" title="Hierarchy">hierarchy</a> with multiple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Police_rank" title="Police rank">ranks</a>. The exact structures and the names of rank vary considerably by country.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uniformed">Uniformed</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Uniform#Police" title="Uniform">Uniform § Police</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brazilian_Federal_Highway_Police.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1241" data-file-width="1645" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Brazilian_Federal_Highway_Police.jpg/220px-Brazilian_Federal_Highway_Police.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Brazilian_Federal_Highway_Police.jpg/330px-Brazilian_Federal_Highway_Police.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Brazilian_Federal_Highway_Police.jpg/440px-Brazilian_Federal_Highway_Police.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brazilian_Federal_Highway_Police.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Brazilian <a href="/wiki/Federal_Highway_Police_(Brazil)" title="Federal Highway Police (Brazil)">Federal Highway Police</a> at work.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Day_274_-_West_Midlands_Police_-_Patrolling_public_transport_connection_points_(8033012427).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="680" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Day_274_-_West_Midlands_Police_-_Patrolling_public_transport_connection_points_%288033012427%29.jpg/220px-Day_274_-_West_Midlands_Police_-_Patrolling_public_transport_connection_points_%288033012427%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Day_274_-_West_Midlands_Police_-_Patrolling_public_transport_connection_points_%288033012427%29.jpg/330px-Day_274_-_West_Midlands_Police_-_Patrolling_public_transport_connection_points_%288033012427%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Day_274_-_West_Midlands_Police_-_Patrolling_public_transport_connection_points_%288033012427%29.jpg/440px-Day_274_-_West_Midlands_Police_-_Patrolling_public_transport_connection_points_%288033012427%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Day_274_-_West_Midlands_Police_-_Patrolling_public_transport_connection_points_(8033012427).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Uniformed police officers of the <a href="/wiki/West_Midlands_Police" title="West Midlands Police">West Midlands Police</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a></div></div></div>
<p>The police who wear <a href="/wiki/Uniform" title="Uniform">uniforms</a> make up the majority of a police service's personnel. Their main duty is to respond to calls to the <a href="/wiki/Emergency_telephone_number" title="Emergency telephone number">emergency telephone number</a>. When not responding to these call-outs, they will do work aimed at preventing crime, such as patrols. The uniformed police are known by varying names such as preventive police, the uniform branch/division, administrative police, order police, the patrol bureau/division or patrol. In Australia and the United Kingdom, patrol personnel are also known as "general duties" officers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bayley-1979_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-bayley-1979-99">[99]</a></sup> Atypically, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>'s preventive police are known as <a href="/wiki/Military_Police_(Brazil)" title="Military Police (Brazil)">Military Police</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>As implied by the name, uniformed police wear uniforms. They perform functions that require an immediate recognition of an officer's legal authority and a potential need for force. Most commonly this means intervening to stop a crime in progress and securing the scene of a crime that has already happened. Besides dealing with crime, these officers may also manage and monitor traffic, carry out <a href="/wiki/Community_policing" title="Community policing">community policing</a> duties, maintain order at public events or carry out searches for <a href="/wiki/Missing_person" title="Missing person">missing people</a> (in 2012, the latter accounted for 14% of police time in the United Kingdom).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> As most of these duties must be available as a <a href="/wiki/24/7_service" title="24/7 service">24/7 service</a>, uniformed police are required to do <a href="/wiki/Shift_work" title="Shift work">shift work</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Detectives">Detectives</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parramatta_201_VE_Commodore_SS_-_Flickr_-_Highway_Patrol_Images_(1).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="966" data-file-width="1286" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Parramatta_201_VE_Commodore_SS_-_Flickr_-_Highway_Patrol_Images_%281%29.jpg/220px-Parramatta_201_VE_Commodore_SS_-_Flickr_-_Highway_Patrol_Images_%281%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Parramatta_201_VE_Commodore_SS_-_Flickr_-_Highway_Patrol_Images_%281%29.jpg/330px-Parramatta_201_VE_Commodore_SS_-_Flickr_-_Highway_Patrol_Images_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Parramatta_201_VE_Commodore_SS_-_Flickr_-_Highway_Patrol_Images_%281%29.jpg/440px-Parramatta_201_VE_Commodore_SS_-_Flickr_-_Highway_Patrol_Images_%281%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Parramatta_201_VE_Commodore_SS_-_Flickr_-_Highway_Patrol_Images_(1).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unmarked_police_car" title="Unmarked police car">Unmarked police cars</a> may be used by detectives or officers to carry out their duties unnoticed by the public, pictured here in <a href="/wiki/Sydney" title="Sydney">Sydney, Australia</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Keskusrikospoliisi_Vantaa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2604" data-file-width="3822" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Keskusrikospoliisi_Vantaa.jpg/220px-Keskusrikospoliisi_Vantaa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Keskusrikospoliisi_Vantaa.jpg/330px-Keskusrikospoliisi_Vantaa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Keskusrikospoliisi_Vantaa.jpg/440px-Keskusrikospoliisi_Vantaa.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Keskusrikospoliisi_Vantaa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/National_Bureau_of_Investigation_(Finland)" title="National Bureau of Investigation (Finland)">National Bureau of Investigation</a> (NBI) headquarters building in <a href="/wiki/Tikkurila" title="Tikkurila">Tikkurila</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vantaa" title="Vantaa">Vantaa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a></div></div></div>
<p>Police <a href="/wiki/Detective" title="Detective">detectives</a> are responsible for investigations and detective work. Detectives may be called Investigations Police, Judiciary/Judicial Police, and Criminal Police. In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UK" title="UK">UK</a>, they are often referred to by the name of their department, the <a href="/wiki/Criminal_Investigation_Department" title="Criminal Investigation Department">Criminal Investigation Department (CID)</a>. Detectives typically make up roughly 15–25% of a police service's personnel.
</p><p>Detectives, in contrast to uniformed police, typically wear 'business attire' in bureaucratic and investigative functions where a uniformed presence would be either a distraction or intimidating, but a need to establish police authority still exists. "Plainclothes" officers dress in attire consistent with that worn by the general public for purposes of blending in.
</p><p>In some cases, police are assigned to work "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Undercover" title="Undercover">undercover</a>", where they conceal their police identity to investigate crimes, such as <a href="/wiki/Organized_crime" title="Organized crime">organized crime</a> or <a href="/wiki/Narcotic" title="Narcotic">narcotics</a> crime, that are unsolvable by other means. In some cases this type of policing shares aspects with <a href="/wiki/Espionage" title="Espionage">espionage</a>.
</p><p>The relationship between detective and uniformed branches varies by country. In the United States, there is high variation within the country itself. Many US police departments require detectives to spend some time on temporary assignments in the patrol division.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> The argument is that rotating officers helps the detectives to better understand the uniformed officers' work, to promote <a href="/wiki/Cross-training" title="Cross-training">cross-training</a> in a wider variety of skills, and prevent "cliques" that can contribute to corruption or other unethical behavior.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Conversely, some countries regard detective work as being an entirely separate profession, with detectives working in separate agencies and recruited without having to serve in uniform. A common compromise in English-speaking countries is that most detectives are recruited from the uniformed branch, but once qualified they tend to spend the rest of their careers in the detective branch.
</p><p>Another point of variation is whether detectives have extra status. In some forces, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Police_Department" title="New York Police Department">New York Police Department</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Police_Department" title="Philadelphia Police Department">Philadelphia Police Department</a>, a regular detective holds a higher rank than a regular police officer. In others, such as <a href="/wiki/Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Law enforcement in the United Kingdom">British police forces</a> and <a href="/wiki/Law_enforcement_in_Canada" title="Law enforcement in Canada">Canadian police forces</a>, a regular detective has equal status with regular uniformed officers. Officers still have to take exams to move to the detective branch, but the move is regarded as being a specialization, rather than a promotion.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Volunteers_and_auxiliary">Volunteers and auxiliary</span></h3>
<p>Police services often include part-time or volunteer officers, some of whom have other jobs outside policing. These may be paid positions or entirely volunteer. These are known by a variety of names, such as reserves, <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_police" title="Auxiliary police">auxiliary police</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Special_constables" title="Special constables">special constables</a>.
</p><p>Other volunteer organizations work with the police and perform some of their duties. Groups in the U.S. including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retired_and_Senior_Volunteer_Program" title="Retired and Senior Volunteer Program">Retired and Senior Volunteer Program</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Community_Emergency_Response_Team" title="Community Emergency Response Team">Community Emergency Response Team</a> and the Boy Scout's <a href="/wiki/Law_Enforcement_Exploring" title="Law Enforcement Exploring">Police Explorers</a> provide training, traffic and crowd control, disaster response and other policing duties. In the U.S., the <a href="/wiki/Volunteers_in_Police_Service" title="Volunteers in Police Service">Volunteers in Police Service</a> program assists over 200,000 volunteers in almost 2,000 programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Volunteers may also work on the support staff. Examples of these schemes are <a href="/wiki/Volunteers_in_Police_Service" title="Volunteers in Police Service">Volunteers in Police Service</a> in the US, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Police_Support_Volunteers" title="Police Support Volunteers">Police Support Volunteers</a> in the UK and <a href="/wiki/Police_Service_(Volunteer_Police)_Amendment_Act_1992" title="Police Service (Volunteer Police) Amendment Act 1992">Volunteers in Policing</a> in New South Wales.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Specialized">Specialized</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SWAT_team_prepared_(4132135578).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="683" data-file-width="1020" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/SWAT_team_prepared_%284132135578%29.jpg/220px-SWAT_team_prepared_%284132135578%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/SWAT_team_prepared_%284132135578%29.jpg/330px-SWAT_team_prepared_%284132135578%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/SWAT_team_prepared_%284132135578%29.jpg/440px-SWAT_team_prepared_%284132135578%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SWAT_team_prepared_(4132135578).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/SWAT" title="SWAT">SWAT</a> team members in the United States</div></div></div>
<p>Specialized preventive and detective groups, or <a href="/wiki/Specialist_investigation_department" title="Specialist investigation department">Specialist Investigation Departments</a> exist within many law enforcement organizations either for dealing with particular types of crime, such as traffic law enforcement, <a href="/wiki/Police_dog" title="Police dog">K9/use of police dogs</a>, crash investigation, <a href="/wiki/Homicide" title="Homicide">homicide</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Fraud" title="Fraud">fraud</a>; or for situations requiring specialized skills, such as <a href="/wiki/Police_diving" title="Police diving">underwater search</a>, <a href="/wiki/Police_aviation" title="Police aviation">aviation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bomb_disposal" title="Bomb disposal">explosive device disposal</a> ("bomb squad"), and <a href="/wiki/Cybercrime" title="Cybercrime">computer crime</a>.
</p><p>Most larger jurisdictions also employ specially selected and trained quasi-<a href="/wiki/Military" title="Military">military</a> <a href="/wiki/Police_tactical_unit" title="Police tactical unit">tactical units</a> armed with military-grade weapons for the purposes of dealing with particularly violent situations beyond the capability of a patrol officer response, including high-risk warrant service and barricaded suspects. In the United States these units go by a variety of names, but are commonly known as <a href="/wiki/SWAT" title="SWAT">SWAT</a> (Special Weapons And Tactics) teams.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Counterinsurgency" title="Counterinsurgency">counterinsurgency</a>-type campaigns, select and specially trained units of police armed and equipped as <a href="/wiki/Light_infantry" title="Light infantry">light infantry</a> have been designated as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Police_field_force" title="Police field force">police field forces</a> who perform <a href="/wiki/Paramilitary" title="Paramilitary">paramilitary</a>-type patrols and ambushes whilst retaining their police powers in areas that were highly dangerous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because their situational mandate typically focuses on removing innocent bystanders from dangerous people and dangerous situations, not violent resolution, they are often equipped with non-lethal tactical tools like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_agents" title="Chemical agents">chemical agents</a>, "<a href="/wiki/Stun_grenade" title="Stun grenade">flashbang</a>" and concussion grenades, and rubber bullets. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specialist_Firearms_Command" title="Specialist Firearms Command">Specialist Firearms Command</a> (CO19)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> of the Metropolitan Police in London is a group of armed police used in dangerous situations including hostage taking, armed robbery/assault and terrorism.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_duties">Administrative duties</span></h3>
<p>Police may have administrative duties that are not directly related to enforcing the law, such as issuing firearms licenses. The extent that police have these functions varies among countries, with police in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">continental European</a> countries handling such tasks to a greater extent than British counterparts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bayley-1979_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-bayley-1979-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vladimir_Putin_in_Khmeimim_Air_Base_in_Syria_(2017-12-11)_19.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1160" data-file-width="1880" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Vladimir_Putin_in_Khmeimim_Air_Base_in_Syria_%282017-12-11%29_19.jpg/220px-Vladimir_Putin_in_Khmeimim_Air_Base_in_Syria_%282017-12-11%29_19.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Vladimir_Putin_in_Khmeimim_Air_Base_in_Syria_%282017-12-11%29_19.jpg/330px-Vladimir_Putin_in_Khmeimim_Air_Base_in_Syria_%282017-12-11%29_19.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Vladimir_Putin_in_Khmeimim_Air_Base_in_Syria_%282017-12-11%29_19.jpg/440px-Vladimir_Putin_in_Khmeimim_Air_Base_in_Syria_%282017-12-11%29_19.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vladimir_Putin_in_Khmeimim_Air_Base_in_Syria_(2017-12-11)_19.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Military_Police" title="Russian Military Police">Russian Military Police</a> officers</div></div></div>
<p>Military police may refer to:
</p>
<ul><li>a section of the <a href="/wiki/Military" title="Military">military</a> solely responsible for policing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armed_forces" title="Armed forces">armed forces</a> (referred to as <a href="/wiki/Provost_(military_police)" title="Provost (military police)">provosts</a>)</li>
<li>a section of the military responsible for policing in both the armed forces and in the civilian population (most <a href="/wiki/Gendarmerie" title="Gendarmerie">gendarmeries</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/National_Gendarmerie" title="National Gendarmerie">French Gendarmerie</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italian</a> <a href="/wiki/Carabinieri" title="Carabinieri">Carabinieri</a>, the Spanish <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guardia_Civil" title="Guardia Civil">Guardia Civil</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portuguese_Republican_National_Guard" title="Portuguese Republican National Guard">Portuguese Republican National Guard</a> also known as GNR)</li>
<li>a section of the military solely responsible for policing the civilian population (such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romanian_Gendarmerie" title="Romanian Gendarmerie">Romanian Gendarmerie</a>)</li>
<li>the civilian preventive police of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brazilian_state" title="Brazilian state">Brazilian state</a> (<a href="/wiki/Military_Police_(Brazil)" title="Military Police (Brazil)">Policia Militar</a>)</li>
<li>a special military law enforcement service, like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Military_Police" title="Russian Military Police">Russian Military Police</a></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious">Religious</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output The specific problem is: <b>Needs more examples as Islam is not the only religion with religious police.</b><span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Police&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> if you can.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">February 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Taliban_beating_woman_in_public_RAWA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="325" data-file-width="450" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Taliban_beating_woman_in_public_RAWA.jpg/220px-Taliban_beating_woman_in_public_RAWA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Taliban_beating_woman_in_public_RAWA.jpg/330px-Taliban_beating_woman_in_public_RAWA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Taliban_beating_woman_in_public_RAWA.jpg/440px-Taliban_beating_woman_in_public_RAWA.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Taliban_beating_woman_in_public_RAWA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two members of the Taliban religious police (Amr bil Ma-roof, or department for the Promotion of Virtue and Prevention of Vice) beating a woman for removing her burqa in public.</div></div></div>
<p>According to World Atlas, there are eight <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic" title="Islamic">Islamic</a> societies that have <a href="/wiki/Religious_police" title="Religious police">religious police</a>, who enforce the application of Islamic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sharia_law" title="Sharia law">Sharia law</a>: Afghanistan, Iran, Iraq, Mauritania, Saudi Arabia, Sudan and Yemen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Their authority may include the power to arrest unrelated men and women caught socializing<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="(February 2021)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, anyone engaged in homosexual behavior or prostitution; to enforce Islamic dress codes, and store closures <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salat" title="Salat">during Islamic prayer time</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asianews.it_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-asianews.it-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>They enforce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_dietary_laws" title="Muslim dietary laws">Muslim dietary laws</a>, prohibit the consumption or sale of <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_beverage" title="Alcoholic beverage">alcoholic beverages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pork" title="Pork">pork</a>, and seize banned consumer products and media regarded as un-Islamic, such as CDs/DVDs of various Western musical groups, television shows and film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asianews.it_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-asianews.it-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-108">[108]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutaween" title="Mutaween">Mutaween</a> actively prevent the practice or proselytizing of non-Islamic religions within Saudi Arabia, where they are banned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asianews.it_107-2"><a href="#cite_note-asianews.it-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_108-2"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:right;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Secret">Secret</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Secret_police" title="Secret police">Secret police</a></div>
<p>Secret police organisations are typically used to suppress dissidents for engaging in non-politically correct communications and activities, which are deemed counter-productive to what the <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">state</a> and related <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_establishment" title="The establishment">establishment</a> promote. Secret police interventions to stop such activities are often illegal, and are designed to debilitate, in various ways, the people targeted in order to limit or stop outright their ability to 
act in a non-politically correct manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> The methods employed may involve <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spying" title="Spying">spying</a>, various acts of deception, <a href="/wiki/Intimidation" title="Intimidation">intimidation</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Framing_(law)" title="Framing (law)">framing</a>, false <a href="/wiki/Imprisonment" title="Imprisonment">imprisonment</a>, false <a href="/wiki/Political_abuse_of_psychiatry" title="Political abuse of psychiatry">incarceration under mental health legislation</a>, and physical <a href="/wiki/Violence" title="Violence">violence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Countries widely reported to use secret police organisations include China<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_State_Security_(China)" title="Ministry of State Security (China)">The Ministry of State Security</a>) and North Korea (<a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_State_Security_(North_Korea)" title="Ministry of State Security (North Korea)">The Ministry of State Security</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="By_country">By country</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Law_enforcement_by_country" title="Law enforcement by country">Law enforcement by country</a></div>
<p>Police forces are usually organized and funded by some level of government. The level of government responsible for policing varies from place to place, and may be at the national, regional or local level. Some countries have police forces that serve the same territory, with their <a href="/wiki/Jurisdiction" title="Jurisdiction">jurisdiction</a> depending on the type of crime or other circumstances. Other countries, such as <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, have a single national police force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>In some places with multiple national police forces, one common arrangement is to have a civilian police force and a paramilitary <a href="/wiki/Gendarmerie" title="Gendarmerie">gendarmerie</a>, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Police_Nationale" title="Police Nationale">Police Nationale</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Gendarmerie" title="National Gendarmerie">National Gendarmerie</a> in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bayley-1979_99-2"><a href="#cite_note-bayley-1979-99">[99]</a></sup> The French policing system spread to other countries through the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley-114">[114]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/French_colonial_empire" title="French colonial empire">French colonial empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SyrGend1_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-SyrGend1-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Congo_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Congo-116">[116]</a></sup> Another example is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polic%C3%ADa_Nacional_(Spain)" title="Policía Nacional (Spain)">Policía Nacional</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guardia_Civil" title="Guardia Civil">Guardia Civil</a> in <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>. In both France and Spain, the civilian force polices urban areas and the paramilitary force polices rural areas. Italy has a similar arrangement with the <a href="/wiki/Polizia_di_Stato" title="Polizia di Stato">Polizia di Stato</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carabinieri" title="Carabinieri">Carabinieri</a>, though their jurisdictions overlap more. Some countries have separate agencies for uniformed police and detectives, such as the <a href="/wiki/Military_Police_(Brazil)" title="Military Police (Brazil)">Military Police</a> and <a href="/wiki/Civil_Police_(Brazil)" title="Civil Police (Brazil)">Civil Police</a> in <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Carabineros_de_Chile" title="Carabineros de Chile">Carabineros</a> and <a href="/wiki/Investigations_Police_of_Chile" title="Investigations Police of Chile">Investigations Police</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>. 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Armenian_Police_Honour_Guard.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="331" data-file-width="381" decoding="async" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Armenian_Police_Honour_Guard.jpeg/220px-Armenian_Police_Honour_Guard.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Armenian_Police_Honour_Guard.jpeg/330px-Armenian_Police_Honour_Guard.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a3/Armenian_Police_Honour_Guard.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Armenian_Police_Honour_Guard.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A wreath laying team from the <a href="/wiki/Police_of_Armenia" title="Police of Armenia">Armenian Police</a>'s Honour Guard Battalion at <a href="/wiki/Tsitsernakaberd" title="Tsitsernakaberd">Tsitsernakaberd</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Other countries have sub-national police forces, but for the most part their jurisdictions do not overlap. In many countries, especially <a href="/wiki/Federalism" title="Federalism">federations</a>, there may be two or more tiers of police force, each serving different levels of government and enforcing different subsets of the law. In <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, the majority of policing is carried out by state (i.e. provincial) police forces, which are supplemented by a federal police force. Though not a federation, the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> has a similar arrangement, where policing is primarily the responsibility of a regional police force and specialist units exist at the national level. In <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Canadian_Mounted_Police" title="Royal Canadian Mounted Police">Royal Canadian Mounted Police</a> (RCMP) are the federal police, while municipalities can decide whether to run a local police service or to contract local policing duties to a larger one. Most urban areas have a local police service, while most rural areas contract it to the RCMP, or to the provincial police in <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>. 
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> has a highly decentralized and fragmented system of law enforcement, with over 17,000 state and local law enforcement agencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> These agencies include local police, county law enforcement (often in the form of a <a href="/wiki/Sheriffs_in_the_United_States" title="Sheriffs in the United States">sheriff's office</a>, or <a href="/wiki/County_police#United_States" title="County police">county police</a>), <a href="/wiki/State_police_(United_States)" title="State police (United States)">state police</a> and <a href="/wiki/Federal_law_enforcement_in_the_United_States" title="Federal law enforcement in the United States">federal law enforcement agencies</a>. Federal agencies, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FBI" title="FBI">FBI</a>, only have jurisdiction over federal crimes or those that involve more than one state. Other federal agencies have jurisdiction over a specific type of crime. Examples include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Federal_Protective_Service" title="United States Federal Protective Service">Federal Protective Service</a>, which patrols and protects government buildings; the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postal_Inspection_Service" title="United States Postal Inspection Service">postal police</a>, which protect postal buildings, vehicles and items; the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Park_Police" title="United States Park Police">Park Police</a>, which protect national parks; and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amtrak_Police" title="Amtrak Police">Amtrak Police</a>, which patrol <a href="/wiki/Amtrak" title="Amtrak">Amtrak</a> stations and trains. There are also some government agencies that perform police functions in addition to other duties, such as the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Coast_Guard" title="United States Coast Guard">Coast Guard</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International">International</span></h2>
<p>Most countries are members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Police_Organization_-_Interpol" title="International Criminal Police Organization - Interpol">International Criminal Police Organization</a> (Interpol), established to detect and fight <a href="/wiki/Transnational_crime" title="Transnational crime">transnational crime</a> and provide for international co-operation and co-ordination of other police activities, such as notifying relatives of the death of foreign nationals. Interpol does not conduct investigations or arrests by itself, but only serves as a central point for information on crime, suspects and criminals. <a href="/wiki/Political_crime" title="Political crime">Political crimes</a> are excluded from its competencies.
</p><p>The terms international policing, transnational policing, and/or global policing began to be used from the early 1990s onwards to describe forms of policing that transcended the boundaries of the sovereign nation-state (Nadelmann, 1993),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nadelmann,_E._A._1993_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nadelmann,_E._A._1993-118">[118]</a></sup> (Sheptycki, 1995).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> These terms refer in variable ways to practices and forms for policing that, in some sense, transcend national borders. This includes a variety of practices, but international police cooperation, criminal intelligence exchange between police agencies working in different nation-states, and police development-aid to weak, failed or failing states are the three types that have received the most scholarly attention.
</p><p>Historical studies reveal that policing agents have undertaken a variety of cross-border police missions for many years (Deflem, 2002).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> For example, in the 19th century a number of European policing agencies undertook cross-border surveillance because of concerns about anarchist agitators and other political radicals. A notable example of this was the occasional surveillance by <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussian</a> police of <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> during the years he remained resident in London. The interests of public police agencies in cross-border co-operation in the control of political radicalism and ordinary law crime were primarily initiated in Europe, which eventually led to the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Interpol" title="Interpol">Interpol</a> before the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>. There are also many interesting examples of cross-border policing under private auspices and by municipal police forces that date back to the 19th century (Nadelmann, 1993).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nadelmann,_E._A._1993_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nadelmann,_E._A._1993-118">[118]</a></sup> It has been established that modern policing has transgressed national boundaries from time to time almost from its inception. It is also generally agreed that in the post–<a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> era this type of practice became more significant and frequent (Sheptycki, 2000).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>Not a lot of empirical work on the practices of inter/transnational information and intelligence sharing has been undertaken. A notable exception is <a href="/wiki/James_Sheptycki" title="James Sheptycki">James Sheptycki</a>'s study of police cooperation in the English Channel region (2002),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> which provides a systematic content analysis of information exchange files and a description of how these transnational information and intelligence exchanges are transformed into police case-work. The study showed that transnational police information sharing was routinized in the cross-Channel region from 1968 on the basis of agreements directly between the police agencies and without any formal agreement between the countries concerned. By 1992, with the signing of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Schengen_Treaty" title="Schengen Treaty">Schengen Treaty</a>, which formalized aspects of police information exchange across the territory of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, there were worries that much, if not all, of this intelligence sharing was opaque, raising questions about the efficacy of the accountability mechanisms governing police information sharing in Europe (Joubert and Bevers, 1996).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>Studies of this kind outside of Europe are even rarer, so it is difficult to make generalizations, but one small-scale study that compared transnational police information and intelligence sharing practices at specific cross-border locations in North America and Europe confirmed that the low visibility of police information and intelligence sharing was a common feature (Alain, 2001).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Intelligence-led_policing" title="Intelligence-led policing">Intelligence-led policing</a> is now common practice in most advanced countries (Ratcliffe, 2007)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ratcliffe,_J._2007_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ratcliffe,_J._2007-125">[125]</a></sup> and it is likely that police intelligence sharing and information exchange has a common morphology around the world (Ratcliffe, 2007).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ratcliffe,_J._2007_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ratcliffe,_J._2007-125">[125]</a></sup> James Sheptycki has analyzed the effects of the new information technologies on the organization of policing-intelligence and suggests that a number of 'organizational pathologies' have arisen that make the functioning of security-intelligence processes in transnational policing deeply problematic. He argues that transnational police information circuits help to "compose the panic scenes of the security-control society".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> The paradoxical effect is that, the harder policing agencies work to produce security, the greater are feelings of insecurity.
</p><p>Police development-aid to weak, failed or failing states is another form of transnational policing that has garnered attention. This form of transnational policing plays an increasingly important role in <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> <a href="/wiki/Peacekeeping" title="Peacekeeping">peacekeeping</a> and this looks set to grow in the years ahead, especially as the international community seeks to develop the rule of law and reform security institutions in States recovering from conflict (Goldsmith and Sheptycki, 2007)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> With transnational police development-aid the imbalances of power between donors and recipients are stark and there are questions about the applicability and transportability of policing models between jurisdictions (Hills, 2009).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>One topic concerns making transnational policing institutions democratically accountable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> According to the Global Accountability Report for 2007 (Lloyd, et al. 2007), Interpol had the lowest scores in its category (IGOs), coming in tenth with a score of 22% on overall accountability capabilities (p. 19).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Equipment">Equipment</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Weapons">Weapons</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:207px;"><a class="image"
